INTRODUCTION
1. This matter has previously been the subject of a preliminary
determination with respect to the nature of the proceedings:
[2018] TASHPT 1.
These reasons concern the determination of the substantive issue between the
parties. In this matter, Dr Tolman has applied, pursuant
to s21(2) of the
Health Practitioners Tribunal Act 2010 (“the Tribunal Act”) for a
review of a decision made under s199(1)
of the Schedule to the Health
Practitioner Regulation National Law (Qld) Act 2009 (“the National
Law”) which has been
adopted as the law of Tasmania by s4 of the Health
Practitioner Regulation National Law (Tasmania) Act 2010.
2. The decision in
respect of which a review is sought is a decision made by a Performance and
Professional Standards Panel (“the
Panel”). The Panel conducted a
hearing over 2 days on 4 and 5 June 2015 and on 10 February 2016 published its
decision and
provided reasons for that decision. The Panel concluded Dr Tolman
behaved in a way that constituted unsatisfactory professional
performance. The
Panel imposed a reprimand together with 23 conditions on Dr Tolman’s
registration. The operation of those
conditions was stayed upon conditions
which were imposed by the Tribunal: see
[2016] TASHPT 3.
3. Dr Tolman’s
position is this Tribunal, pursuant to s202 of the National Law, substitute
another decision for the Panel’s
decision. The decision which she submits
the Tribunal should substitute for the Panel’s decision is the Tribunal
should find
Dr Tolman has no case to answer and that no further action is to be
taken as is provided for by s191(1)(a) of the National Law.
If contrary to that
submission the Tribunal finds Dr Tolman has behaved in a way that constitutes
unsatisfactory professional performance
then she seeks to make submissions on
penalty, based on the Tribunal’s decision, before the penalty is
determined.
4. The respondent’s position is the Panel’s decision
both in respect of its finding that Dr Tolman engaged in unsatisfactory
professional performance and the sanctions it imposed was the correct preferable
result in respect of the matter to which its decision
relates. It submits that
presented with essentially the same material as the Panel had before it, but
having regard to the additional
opinions provided since the Panel delivered its
determination which have been obtained from Dr Walker, Dr Barbato, Professor
and Dr Chapman, and the evidence received viva voce from Professor Bell and
Dr Chapman and after making its own findings in respect
of all of that material
it says the Tribunal should come to the same result. Accordingly the respondent
submits the Tribunal should
confirm the Panel’s decision subject to the
modifications made to that decision by the Tribunal by its orders of 2 May 2016.
In addition it is submitted the Tribunal should order the removal of the stay
imposed by the Tribunal’s order of 2 May 2016.
The History of These Proceedings
5. Dr Tolman’s application to this Tribunal was filed on
8 March 2016. It is regrettable that from that point it took some
before her application was heard. The first thing that occurred was Dr
Tolman’s application for a stay was heard
on 18 April 2016 and a decision
with respect to that application was handed down on 2 May 2016.
June 2016 the first directions hearing was held. A timetable was set for the
provision by the respondent of all the material
considered by the Panel, that
the applicant was to deliver to the Tribunal and the respondent copies of all
material upon which she
intended to rely at the hearing save for any documents
already forming part of the material which was before the Panel and she was
to identify any documents not intended to be relied upon or documents to which
an objection will be made to their tender. The
respondent was also given time
to identify any documents or material it would not intend to rely upon at the
hearing of this matter.
7. On 8 June 2016 the Tribunal received from the
respondent almost 9,000 pages of material. Thereafter on 31 August 2016 Dr
filed and served reports and/or proofs of Dr Barbato and Clinical
Professor Bell and on 5 September 2016 she filed and served a proof
8. On 17 October 2016, Dr Tolman, on her own account, enquired about
a mediation. The respondent agreed to that course and a date
of 12 April 2017
was organised which appears from the file to be the earliest date on which
everybody was available to attend such
a conference. The mediation did not
resolve any of the issues in dispute between the parties.
9. The first
directions hearing I conducted in this matter was held on 3 May 2017. Prior to
that directions hearing both the solicitor
for Dr Tolman, on 24 April 2017, and
the solicitor for AHPRA, on 26 April 2017, indicated they did not object to me
hearing this
matter despite my previous firm, Dobson Mitchell & Allport, a
firm at which I had been a principal, acting for Dr Tolman for
the best part of
2016. I raised this issue again in the presence of counsel on 3 May 2017 and
both indicated that their respective
clients did not object to me hearing the
matter. I advised that I knew nothing about the matter until I had considered
the Tribunal’s
file prior to that directions hearing. At that directions
hearing the respondent confirmed its previously expressed view in an email
June 2016 that it wished the Tribunal to be comprised of the Chair and two
professional members. Dr Tolman’s position
on this point was she was
content for me to hear her application alone but noted the respondent’s
request and was therefore
content with two professional members rather than one
professional member and a community member. A list of potential Tribunal
was therefore to be provided to each party.
10. At the resumed
directions hearing on 7 August 2017 counsel for Dr Tolman indicated his view was
the list which had been circulated
did not comply with s12(1) of the Tribunal
Act and that needed to be complied with first before we moved on to s12(2) which
the list which had been circulated appears to comply with. Counsel for
the respondent tended to agree with that position. I therefore
directed AHPRA
to provide to the parties and the Tribunal a list of Tasmanian practitioners
pursuant to s12(1) within 14 days. There
was then a discussion about the
documents which the respondent wished to rely on and which Dr Tolman objected
to. It was at that
time counsel for Dr Tolman indicated he would not be
available to conduct Dr Tolman’s application until February or March 2018
and he therefore suggested we look at a date at that time for the hearing of
this matter. I indicated we could set that time aside
and once we had the list
of Tasmanian practitioners this matter could be relisted and I would then hear
the parties as to who, on
that list, was appropriate for appointment to hear
this matter.
11. The next directions hearing was conducted on 4 September
2017. It was determined after some argument there was insufficient information
respondent indicated he would have his solicitor
undertake relevant enquiries.
Once suitable practitioners were identified they then needed to be contacted to
determine their availability.
I directed AHPRA to make enquiries as to the
availability and suitability of the seven practitioners on that list who Dr
considered were suitable. The directions hearing was adjourned to enable
those enquiries to be conducted.
12. The next directions hearing was
conducted on 2 October 2017. It was indicated at that time by counsel for the
respondent there
was only one general practitioner on the list who was willing
and available and given her response to AHPRA’s enquiry, she
suitable. The previous list was then referred to and Dr Tolman’s counsel
indicated he was prepared to treat that
list as a s12(2) list. Counsel for the
respondent indicated AHPRA would make enquiries of the medical practitioners on
The matter was pencilled in for hearing on 19 February 2018 and the
directions hearing was adjourned until 23 October 2017.
13. On 23 October
2017 counsel for both parties indicated there were three possible members; two
on the Tasmanian list and one on
the s12(2) list. It was submitted I had to
choose from the Tasmanian list first and if I was satisfied about both
candidates on
that list then there was no need to go to the National list. Dr
Tolman’s position was all three were suitable. It was not
clear whether
one of the Tasmanian practitioners was available to commence this hearing on 19
February 2018 so the matter was again
adjourned for those enquiries to be made.
After considering the information I had been provided I considered Dr Davis met
the requirements
set out in s11(2)(a)-(d) and therefore he was formally
appointed as a professional member of this Tribunal in these proceedings.
was also directed that the matter be listed for a prehearing conference
approximately one month prior to 19 February 2018.
14. In late October 2017
the parties revisited the medical practitioners on the Tasmanian list. The
respondent proposed Dr Williams
as a professional member, she having previously
been excluded. Dr Tolman’s solicitor indicated his client did not object
the appointment of Dr Williams. After determining her availability and after
being satisfied she met the requirements set out in
s11(2)(a)-(d) I formally
appointed Dr Williams as a professional member of this Tribunal on 2 November
15. The matter was then set down for a prehearing conference on 22
January 2017 and for hearing on 19 February 2018. At the prehearing
directions were made as to the filing and service of any witness statements to
be relied upon by either party. The witness
schedule, the need for video links
and the provision of documents to the Tribunal Members on a USB (given the
volume of them) were
discussed. As were the objections which Dr Tolman intended
to take as to the admissibility of some of the material which the Panel
considered. There was also a discussion with respect to Dr Tolman’s
objection in respect of each of the witnesses which
the respondent proposed to
call whose evidence does not touch upon any of the patients. The matter was
adjourned for hearing to
19 February 2018 at 10:00am. It was heard on 19 to 22
February 2018 and on 26 February 2018.
16. During the hearing an order was
made that the names of the patients be de-identified. In these reasons each of
the twelve patients
will be referred to as Patient 1 to Patient 12
respectively.
17. The manner in which Dr Tolman’s application was
conducted is worthy of comment. In a letter from her solicitor to the Tribunal
of 1 February 2018 the following statement appears:
avoid a miscarriage of justice, Dr Tolman maintains her objection to any member
of the Tribunal considering the documents
contained within that file prior to
those documents being tendered at the
18. An email from her solicitor to the Tribunal of 16 February
2018 is in the following terms:
“Mr McTaggart SC has
started to review the Respondent’s opening submission as prepared by Mr
Jackson SC.
Objection is taken to the submission, which makes express reference to a
summary of evidence the Respondent knows has been objected
to. Accordingly, the
Applicant objects to the Tribunal considering the Respondent’s submissions
prior to a determination of
the admissibility of the
19. As a result of this correspondence the Tribunal did no
reading prior to the hearing. Accordingly the Tribunal’s consideration
the voluminous documentation which it considered did not take place until after
the hearing and at times when each of the Tribunal
members were available. The
Tribunal’s determination in this matter was therefore significantly

21. The Tasmanian Board of the Medical Board of Australia undertook
an investigation and determined on 13 February 2015 that a Performance
Professional Panel be established.

advised Dr Tolman of the Medical Board of Australia’s decision to
establish a Performance
and Professional Standards Panel pursuant to s182 of the
National Law. The letter identified allegations concerning Dr Tolman’s
practise of medicine in respect of twelve patients. By specific reference to
documents and witness statements it provided detailed
reasons for establishing
23. By letter of 12 May 2015 Dr Tolman was given notice pursuant

B. The Allegations
25. The Panel considered whether nine issues arose in relation
to some or any of the twelve patients and those issues are set out
in paragraph
3.1 of the Panel’s decision as follows:
(a) Whether Dr Tolman’s record keeping and
documentation were unsatisfactory;
(b) Whether Dr Tolman’s decision
making was excessively hasty and not fully considered;
(c) Whether Dr Tolman
failed to adequately discuss, communicate and consult with
(d) Whether Dr Tolman accelerated treatment
inappropriately;
(e) Whether Dr Tolman sanctioned the aggressive and
inappropriate use of medications;
(f) Whether Dr Tolman was too quick to
assess patients as lacking decision making capacity;
(g) Whether Dr Tolman
failed to properly make a diagnosis, or was inflexible in her
(h) Whether there were diagnostic uncertainties regarding the
cessation of antibiotics; and
(i) Whether treatment decisions were well
founded on medical grounds.

matters to which the Panel’s decision relates.

its decision Dr Tolman did not seek leave to be legally represented
hearing. Despite that the decision notes her legal representative was present
for most of the hearing as a support person
but did not take part in the
proceedings. In her legal representative’s absence, Dr Tolman was
supported by a colleague.
It seems from the Panel’s reasons at paragraph
4.2 no applications were made by Dr Tolman during the proceedings and the Panel
took into account the documentation mentioned in paragraph 28. Paragraph 10.3 of
the Panel’s reasons discloses the Panel members
reviewed the medical notes
of the 12 patients and discussed each of them with Dr Tolman during the hearing.
The Tribunal notes that
of the five Panel members, three were medical
practitioners and of those three, two were
specialists

32. The Panel then considered the standard of conduct
reasonably expected of a medical practitioner of an equivalent level of training
or experience, which in this case is an experienced geriatrician. It then
referred to Good Medicine Practice: A Code of Conduct
for Doctors in Australia
(“the Code”). The purpose of the Code is set out in paragraph 1.1
as follows:
describes what is expected of all doctors registered to practise medicine in
Australia. It sets out the principles that characterise
good medical practice
and makes explicit the standards of ethical and professional conduct expected of
doctors by their professional
peers and the community...This Code does not set
new standards. It brings together, into a single Australian Code, standards
have long been at the core of medical
33. At paragraph 1.2 the Code says it is used for the following
• To support individual doctors in the challenging task
of providing good medical care and fulfilling their professional roles,
provide a framework to guide professional judgement.
• To assist the
Medical Board of Australia in its role of protecting the public, by setting and
maintaining standards of medical
practice against which a doctor’s
professional conduct can be evaluated. If a doctor’s professional conduct
varies significantly
from the standard, that doctor should be prepared to
explain and justify his or her decisions and actions. Serious or repeated
to meet these standards may have consequences for a doctor’s
medical registration.
• As an additional resource for a range of uses
that contribute to enhancing the culture of medical professionalism in the
health system; for example, in medical education, orientation,
induction and supervision of junior doctors and international medical
and by administrators and policy makers in hospitals, health services and other
institutions.

THE STATUTORY SCHEME AND ROLE OF THE TRIBUNAL
37. Section 182 of the National Law provides for the
establishment of a Performance and Professional Standards Panel. It provides,
by subsection (1):
National Board may establish a performance and professional standards panel if
(a) the Board reasonably believes, because of a notification or for any other
reason, that –
(i) the way a registered health practitioner
practises the health profession is or may be unsatisfactory; or
(ii) the registered health practitioner’s professional conduct is or
may be unsatisfactory; and
(b) the Board decides it is necessary or appropriate for the matter to be
referred to a panel.”
38. Section 185 deals with the procedure of the Panel and it
“(1) Subject to this
Division, a panel may decide its own procedures.
(2) A panel is required to observe the principles of natural justice but is
not bound by the rules of evidence.
(3) A panel may have regard to –
(a) a report prepared by an assessor about the registered health practitioner
or student; and
(b) any other information the panel considers relevant to the hearing of the
39. Section 191(1)(b)(i) permits a panel to decide, after
hearing a matter about a registered health practitioner, that the practitioner
has behaved in a way that constitutes unsatisfactory professional performance.
40. Section 192 of the National Law requires the Panel to give notice of its
decision to the National Board who must in turn give
notice to the registered
health practitioner and if the hearing related to a notification that led to the
establishment of the panel
under s181(1)(a), notice is to be given to the
notifier. Section 192(3) requires the notice provided to the registered health
practitioner
must state the decision and the reasons for the decision.
B. Appeals from the Panel and Powers of the Tribunal
41. Sections 199(1)(i) and (k) provide that a person, who is
the subject of a decision by a panel to impose a condition or, who is
a reprimand, may appeal that decision to the appropriate responsible tribunal.
Section 199(2) provides the appropriate
responsible tribunal for a decision to
take performance action in relation to a registered health practitioner is the
responsible
tribunal for the participating jurisdiction in which the behaviour
the subject of the decision occurred. So that is, in this case,
the Tribunal
established in Tasmania under the Tribunal Act.
42. Section 17 of the
Tribunal Act provides for the jurisdiction of this Tribunal as follows:
A reference in the National Law to an appeal against a decision is, for an
appeal in accordance with that Law to the Tribunal,
a reference to a review of
the decision as provided under this Act.
(2) The jurisdiction, conferred by the National Law
on the Tribunal as the responsible tribunal, to hear and decide an appeal
a decision is to be taken to be jurisdiction conferred on the Tribunal
to hear and decide under this Act a review of a decision.
(3) The Tribunal may exercise the jurisdiction,
conferred by the National Law on the Tribunal as the responsible tribunal, to
and decide an appeal against a decision if a person has, under
section 21(2)
, applied to the Tribunal for a review of the decision.
(4) In exercising the jurisdiction, conferred by
the National Law on the Tribunal as the responsible tribunal, to hear and decide
an appeal against a decision, the Tribunal may exercise the powers, and perform
the functions, conferred on the Tribunal by this
Act or the National
43. Section 21(2) of the Tribunal Act provides: “A person
who is the subject of a decision referred to in s199(1) of the National
who may, under that Law, appeal to the responsible tribunal, may apply to the
Tribunal for a review of the decision.”
That is, this section provides
for the process by which an appeal is to be brought and expressly characterises
the proceedings as
involving “a review” by the Tribunal of a
“decision”; that is the appeal is brought by way of an application
to the Tribunal for a review of, in this case, a decision of the Panel.
Therefore the appeal under s199 proceeds on the basis there
has already been a
44. Section 27(1) of the Tribunal Act provides that if an
application is made under s21(2) for a review of a decision and the applicant
has been given, by the person who made the decision, a statement of reasons for
the decision, the applicant must provide the statement
to the Tribunal.
Therefore the reasons for the decision which Dr Tolman has applied to this
Tribunal to review must be supplied
by her to the Tribunal.
45. Division 2
of Part 5 of the Tribunal Act deals with the nature of the proceedings before
the Tribunal. Section 28 provides:
purpose of a review by the Tribunal of a decision is to produce the correct and
preferable result in respect of the
matter to which the decision
(2) The Tribunal must hear and decide a review of a decision by way of a
fresh hearing on the merits of the matter to which the decision
46. Section 202 of the National Law sets out what decision or
decisions a Tribunal can make after hearing the appeal under s199.
Section 202
“(1) After hearing the
matter, the responsible tribunal may –
(a) confirm the appellable decision; or
(b) amend the appellable decision; or
(c) substitute another decision for the appellable decision.
(2) In substituting another decision for the
appellable decision, the responsible tribunal has the same powers as the entity
made the appellable decision.”
C. The Question for this Tribunal
47. These proceedings are only concerned with whether, in
respect of the care exercised by Dr Tolman in her dealings with any of the
twelve patients, she failed to exercise the standard of knowledge, skill or
judgement reasonably expected of a health practitioner
of an equivalent level of
training or experience, in one or more of the ways alleged in the notice of the
Panel’s hearing given
to Dr Tolman under s184 of the National Law.
Accordingly these proceedings are not concerned with any allegation of
misconduct or
impropriety. In fact the respondent in both its written and oral
submissions made no allegations of professional misconduct or unprofessional
conduct against Dr Tolman.
48. It is noted the Panel can find Dr Tolman
behaved in a way that constituted unprofessional conduct (National Law:
s191(1)(b)(ii))
and the Tribuanl can therefore make that finding (National Law:
s202(2)). The respondent has not asked the Tribunal to do so.
D. The Relevant Standard
49. Section 5 of the National Law defines unsatisfactory
professional performance in the following terms:
unsatisfactory
professional performance
, of a registered health practitioner, means
the knowledge, skill or judgment possessed, or care exercised by, the
practitioner in
the practice of the health profession in which the practitioner
is registered is below the standard reasonably expected of a health
practitioner
of an equivalent level of training or
experience.”
50. It is therefore the standard which the Tribunal concludes
is reasonably to be expected of a hypothetical health practitioner of
equivalent level of training or experience to that possessed by Dr Tolman
against which her professional performance, or more
specifically the care
exercised by her, in respect of the twelve patients in question is to be
51. The Tribunal may be assisted in determining what that standard
is by having regard to the following:
(a) the documented standards included amongst the papers for
the Tribunal;
(b) the evidence of those who practised in the same area at the
relevant times and who were able to observe Dr Tolman and more specifically
were able to observe and provide evidence about her dealings with one or more of
the twelve patients.
52. Although the Tribunal is not bound by the rules of evidence
it might be said the central rule, now provided for in
Evidence Act
, should guide the Tribunal’s approach. That is the Tribunal may and
should have regard to any evidence that if it were accepted,
could rationally
affect, directly or indirectly, the assessment of the probability of the
existence of a fact in issue in the proceedings.
53. The facts in issue in
these proceedings are set out in paragraphs 25 and 27 above. It might be said
then the Tribunal may have
regard to evidence in the various statements from
witnesses that comment on general observations of Dr Tolman’s practices
by those witnesses together with their observations insofar as they compare
her practice to those of others they have observed and
worked with; that is
these observations go outside the care of the twelve patients.
Tribunal can also take into account the evidence of experts whose opinion
evidence is intended to assist the Tribunal to come
to an understanding of what
the relevant standard is viz. whether, based on informed and objective analysis
of the patients’
records, it can be said the standard was met by Dr Tolman
in respect of the twelve patients.
55. The Tribunal can also take into
account the training, knowledge and experience of the professional members of
this Tribunal.
It was suggested by counsel for the respondent the very purpose
of a professional specialist tribunal is to provide it with the advantage
being able to bring its own expertise to its deliberations. It was submitted
that is one of the reasons s37(2)(b) of the Tribunal
Act expressly permits the
Tribunal to inform itself on any matter in any way it considers
appropriate.
56. It is clear that a specialist tribunal is not required or
obliged to accept and apply evidence from other health practitioners,
acknowledged to be of good repute and competence, as to what an appropriate
standard of conduct or performance is in any
given situation: Kudelka v Medical
Complaints Tribunal
[2004] TASSC 31
. Of course a tribunal may
accept and apply that evidence and can be informed of the acceptability or
otherwise of certain practices
or conduct and the existence of a
“majority” and “minority” view point of acceptable
conduct by evidence
the cogency of which it accepts: Qidwai v Brown [1984] NSWLR
100 at 105. It is clear from Fletcher v Queensland Nursing Council
[2011] 1 Qd
[2009] QCA 364
a tribunal is required to form its own opinions and make value
judgements that because of their very nature and the specialist nature
tribunals, courts will ordinarily be reluctant to disturb even where the issue
is one of alleged misconduct, rather than professional
performance. In that
case, Chesterman JA said at [86]:
Tribunal is a specialist one. Its expressions of opinion that conduct does or
does not amount to unsatisfactory professional
conduct should be given
considerable weight and not disturbed in the absence of demonstrated error in
the process of reasoning, or
fact finding, or the application of the statutory
definition to the facts. This I understand to be the orthodox approach to
from specialist tribunals.”
E. The Onus of Proof
57. The Panel found Dr Tolman practised in a way that
constituted unsatisfactory professional performance. It imposed a reprimand
conditions on her practice. Dr Tolman has appealed against the finding that she
behaved in a way that constitutes unsatisfactory
professional performance, the
imposition of a reprimand and the imposition of conditions on her registration
as a medical practitioner.
The respondent’s position with respect to onus
was there is probably no onus of proof in these proceedings because they are
strictly adversarial and do not involve allegations of impropriety or misconduct
but instead involve an assessment of the professional
performance of Dr Tolman
measured against the standard to be expected of her peers in circumstances where
the respondent takes a
position in which it advances no specific allegations of
misconduct in the Tribunal. This was the position before the Panel where
respondent simply put all relevant material before the decision maker. The
Board was not a party to, or legally represented,
in the proceedings before the
Panel and is only a party to these proceedings by virtue of s200(b)(ii) of the
National Law.
58. This is to be contrasted to the situation where the Board
brings proceedings in the Tribunal which allege professional misconduct
unsatisfactory professional performance: see Medical Board of Australia v
[2017] WASCA 64
(2017) 51 WAR 32
and Singh v Nursing and Midwifery Board of Australia
[2015] VSC 576
59. Who has the onus and, if there is an onus,
need not be determined in these proceedings. The Tribunal takes the view that
to justify a finding of unsatisfactory performance it must be satisfied
as to that matter on the balance of probabilities; however
the degree of
persuasion required is affected by the seriousness of the allegations made. In
this case the allegations set out in
paragraphs 25 and 27 are serious. The
Tribunal must actually be persuaded of the correctness of the matters of
complaint. As Dixon
J said in Briginshaw v Briginshaw
[1938] HCA 34
at 361-362:
truth is that, when the law requires proof of any fact, the tribunal must feel
an actual persuasion of its occurrence or
existence before it can be found. It
cannot be found as a result of a mere mechanical comparison of probabilities
independently
of any belief in its reality...Except upon criminal issues to be
proved by the prosecution, it is enough that the affirmative of
an allegation is
made out to the reasonable satisfaction of the tribunal. But reasonable
satisfaction is not a state of mind that
is attained or established
independently of the nature and consequences of the fact or facts to be proved.
The seriousness of an
allegation made, the inherent unlikelihood of an
occurrence of a given description, or the gravity of the consequences flowing
a particular finding are considerations which must affect the answer to the
question whether the issue has been proved to the reasonable
satisfaction of the
tribunal. In such matters ‘reasonable satisfaction’ should not be
produced by inexact proofs, indefinite
testimony or indirect
references.”
60. In the context of disciplinary proceedings it is
appropriate for a tribunal to adopt the approach of requiring that it be
“comfortably
satisfied” of the facts in issue: Karakatsanis &
Anor v Racing Victoria Limited
[2013] VSCA 305
(2013) 42 VR 176

Associate Professor Alan Sandford

• Dr Tolman lied when asked in a Mortality and Morbidity
meeting within her clinical service as to whether the death of Patient
been referred to the Coroner.
• In respect of Dr Tolman’s
management of patients he said:
have also become particularly concerned with the nature of Dr Tolman’s
practice in respect to the management of patients
coming into her service being
rapidly and ‘pragmatically’ assessed as palliative and then managed
with an end result
of death. The decisions surrounding these decisions may be a
result of a desire to organizationally expedite patient throughput.
expeditious decision-making is organizationally desirable in times of pressure
for beds but may not take into account significant
elements of geriatric care,
which aims to manage reversible conditions associated with
In respect of this allegation he says the following:
“...Patient 2, a
patient who may have had a reversible condition of low sodium who was treated
with palliation and a syringe
driver. In this case others in the team had
agreed with the provisional assessment but they were dismissed. An autocratic
was then made by Dr Tolman and the Registrar Dr Butcherine was severely
admonished...
Such autocratic decision making...appears motivated by expedience and a
desire to please the Organisation and the families in relieving
their suffering
rather than pain and suffering of the patient; tending to overlook or ignore any
potential reversible conditions
and instead favour
palliation.”
He concluded:
“I believe Dr Tolman
is practicing
in a manner that constitutes risk to her
patients...”

patients identified by the Panel at paragraph 3.2 in its
notification has not been taken into account by the Tribunal. As is clear from

and/or opinions with respect to Dr Tolman’s clinical practice where there

to an issue considered by the panel is of little weight
and is put to one side.
Dr Catherine Yelland
183. In 2013 Dr Yelland was the Director of Medicine and Older
Persons Service, Redcliff Hospital in Queensland. In conjuction with
Tulloch who is a geriatrician at Eastern Health Melbourne, she carried out a
review of aged care services at the Royal Hobart
Hospital in October 2013. That
review was undertaken at the request of the Acting Chief Executive Officer of
THO – South and
the involvement of Dr Yelland and Dr Tulloch was arranged
with the assistance of the Chief Executive Officer of the Royal Australasian
College of Physicians.
184. In her report, Dr Yelland made the following
conclusions which the respondent said were relevant to this case and which were
based on a review of 22 “cases of concern” which were submitted for
consideration in her review:
(a) Cause of death on death certificate appears to have been
inaccurate in a number of cases:
• Renal failure, when last biochemistry did not support
• Myocardial infarct, when death was sudden and unexpected and
should have been reported to the Coroner.
(b) Deaths not reported to Coroner – e.g. death after
a femoral fracture, presumably after a fall.
(c) Fractured femurs treated
with analgesia, when standard management is surgical pinning unless patient is
unlikely to survive surgery.
(d) Inadequate assessment or treatment of acute
and potentially reversible conditions.
(e) Palliative or end of life care
approach, either without reason or without documentation. At times, the
decision to move to end
of life care appears to have been made earlier than
(f) Lack of consultation with family.
185. Dr Yelland reported that:
Tolman’s colleagues had the impression that she would adopt a palliative
approach very early in the admission, when
further investigation and treatment
would have been expected. She used the phrase ‘need to be
realistic’, appeared to
regard a hip fracture as a terminal event, and
said that nursing home care was an end of life event. She appeared to transfer
who were nearing the end of life, from other units, despite the
existence of palliative care service in the hospital, or the patient
normally have remained in the treating
186. In addition Dr Yelland said:
“Junior doctors were
concerned about the doses of medications given, and the use of syringe drivers
(to deliver a constant subcutaneous
or intravenous dose of medication, such as
analgesia or sedation)...
The use of opiates, midazolam and large doses of antipsychotic medications is
not recommended, nor is it accepted practice. Use of
syringe drivers is only
indicated where there are major pain issues and the patient is unable to take
oral medication.
From the interviews, other clinicians conform to usual clinical practice. In
the limited number of cases reviewed it appears that
at times, Dr Tolman treated
patients who have clinical presentations consistent with
with syringe drivers,
administering morphine and midazolam. She did not ask for psychogeriatric
consultations for difficult patients.
There is little doubt that in some cases
this shortened their life.”
187. Dr Yelland also noted:
“Concerns about
[Dr Tolman’s]
clinical practice included ‘aggressive terminal
sedation’ of patients, inadequate evaluation of clinical problems and
insufficient time allowed for improvement in
188. In the third last paragraph of her report, Dr Yelland
comments upon Dr Tolman’s professional performance in the following
“The management of
frail older patients, particularly those with dementia, appears, within the
limits of this review (which
did not directly observe clinical care) to fall
outside the standards of evidence based practice, and that which would be
by her peers.”
189. Dr Yelland says that she and Dr Tulloch, as reviewers,
were able to access the notes of the cases which were submitted for their
consideration and also had comments from other clinicians but they said this
would introduce a bias so their comments on the cases
“are aggregated and
general, and are an impression from a peer, rather than a more detailed analysis
of each case”.
190. Dr Tolman says that of the 12 patients the subject
of the Panel’s decision there were four which fell within this review,
namely Patient 3, Patient 5, Patient 8 and Patient 12. Having considered each
patient’s medical record and Dr Yelland’s
report and noting that the
initials used in Dr Yelland’s report for three of the four patients are
not consistent with those
patients’ actual initials, the Tribunal is
satisfied the four patients mentioned in Dr Yelland’s report are the
of the Panel’s decision. Even though the use of initials vary,
the Tribunal examined the comments of Dr Yelland with respect
to three of the
patients and cross referenced those comments with the medical
191. As to Patient 3, the comment is the death certificate points to
a condition for which no evidence was noted in the medical record.
No weight is
given to this comment because it was not an issue before the Panel.
relation to Patient 5, Dr Tolman submits the Panel’s findings with respect
to this patient related to the aggressive
use of medication and Dr
Yelland’s comment that it was unclear why the patient needed analgesia and
sedation goes more to record
keeping. It is clear the Panel’s findings
with respect to this patient were more than submitted by Dr Tolman; see
31. One of the findings in relation to this patient by the Panel is a
lack of a diagnosis while another was the aggressive and inappropriate
medications. It might be thought Dr Yelland’s comment about it being
unclear why the patient needed analgesia and sedation
goes to a lack of
diagnosis and in those circumstances it is suggestive of an aggressive and
inappropriate use of medication. This
point is given no weight by the Tribunal
because of what Dr Yelland and Dr Tulloch say at paragraph 189. Having
considered the records
ourselves we will make our own determination about these
193. As to Patient 8 Dr Yelland’s comment is not relevant to
the Panel’s findings with respect to the poor notes and a
discussions, consultation and communication with family. It may be relevant to
the finding with respect to whether Dr Tolman’s
treatment decisions were
well founded on medical grounds, however we have given no weight to the comment
because of what Dr Yelland
and Dr Tulloch say at paragraph 189.
relation to Patient 12 the comment is in relation to not reporting the death to
the Coroner. That was not an issue considered
by the Panel and is given no
195. The Tribunal agrees with Dr Tolman’s submission that any
other conclusions drawn by Dr Yelland, unless related to the 12
patients, are of
little or no weight. The Tribunal also agrees Dr Yelland’s letter to Ms
Holden, which is attached to her
report, is irrelevant to the issues before the

196. In his letter to AHPRA dated 24 July 2014, Dr Gangell says

concerns regarding Dr Tolman’s approach to end of life care have been in
relation to the time frame over which major
changes in clinical goals were made
and the opportunity for family members to digest these changes before they were
End of life care is a very complex area of medicine with substantive grey
areas regarding what could be determined as best practice.
As a clinician, Dr
Tolman often appeared proactive in adopting a palliative approach. I do not
hold objection to Dr Tolman’s
approach in general but did hold concerns
that on occasion, the decision to move away from quantity of life
(interventionist) medicine
to symptom management (palliative) medicine was made
with haste such that primary care providers and relatives did not have time
effectively contribute to the decision

In relation to Patient 6, Dr Gangell said:

alter the outcome of a likely terminal event was probably quite reasonable but

illness – hence her transfer to an acute care facility

198. Dr Gangell concluded with two observations; the first was

“12. On a number of

medical treatment are often perceived by health
workers to be too difficult, and hence are avoided. Families and nurses were at
times reported to be grateful, but I heard rumours that some general practice
colleagues were not happy with process, and sometimes
could certainly be said that Dr Tolman was energetic in her palliative care
interventions in the aged care sector, and was seen
by many as the go-to person

Dr Michael Chapman
239. As part of its investigation the Board obtained a report
from Dr Chapman who is a geriatrician and palliative medicine specialist
with Calvary Health Care in the ACT as a staff specialist. At the time of his
initial report (pages 239-287 of the court
book) he was engaged in a PHD through
Monash University which focused on the ethics and experience of decision making
for persons
with dementia and their carers. He sits on the steering committees
of several national programs which focus on providing palliative
care to older
persons including the Palliative Approach toolkit rollout to Aged Care
Facilities through Brisbane South Palliative
Care Collaborative and the
Specialist Palliative Care and Advanced Care Planning Advisory Services projects
being run through a consortium
including Austin Health, ANZSPM, PCA and other
organisations. He is also a council member and secretary for the Australian and
Zealand Society of Palliative Medicine and a council member for Palliative
Care Australia’s National Policy Advisory Council.
240. Dr Chapman was
provided with all of the notifier’s documents together with the statements
obtained by AHPRA from those
who had worked with Dr Tolman either at the Royal
Hobart Hospital but more particularly on the OPU. Those statements are from Dr
Butcherine, Dr Lees, Dr Cleary, Professor Ashby, Dr Nicklason, Dr Dunbabin, Dr
Chamberlen, Dr Skelton and Professor Jose. He was
also provided with the
letters of Dr Bakas and Dr Morrissey. He considered eighteen separate patient
records including those twelve
patients which were considered by the Panel. His
comments with respect to the six patients who were not the subject of the
determination were redacted from his report.
241. A summary of
his comments and opinions from the clinical cases appears at pages 285 and 286
of the court book in the following
of the situation and cases described previously rely on summarised reporting of
conversations and situations rather than
direct reporting. Given the complexity
of the ethics and communications implied in some of these situations it may be
that the notes
and recounting available do not do justice to the situations as
they were. This report then acknowledges what can’t be known
data that is available, but has sought to provide balanced comment on what is
available. It is also timely to acknowledge
that this report cannot comment on
Dr Tolman’s practice in a general way, but only in these instances which
have been reported,
which presumably are indicative and yet make up only a
fraction of the cases which she has been involved in.
In general terms I feel that there are a number of concerns which can be
identified in a number of areas of Dr Tolman’s clinical
and professional
In regards to her clinical practice from the case examples that have been
provided Dr JT’s approach often seems one which assumes
a position that a
‘comfort care’, palliative approach is the only way forward. A
clinician as experienced as Dr Tolman
would well know the flexibility required
to manage complex clinical situations in patients with as many issues as the
ones mentioned.
This flexibility is not apparent in the cases, nor
interestingly is it apparent in the way Dr Tolman discusses these cases in her
response letter, though the mitigating factor of the adversarial nature of this
process needs to be taken into account. In the cases
discussed there seems
often to be a ‘right’ way forward for Dr Tolman. How this approach
and her conviction affects
the perspectives of families and patients she is
talking to is impossible to gauge though it is notable that in several cases
understandings
reached in conversation with Dr Tolman are directly recanted when
the same events are discussed with others. It is also notable
that on a number
of occasions the patient themselves seems to have been left out of discussions
and decisions in regards to their
own management without clarity of why this
should be the case. Part of this could be due to Dr Tolman’s habit of
documenting
only the results of discussions rather than what has proceeded
within them, though this is not knowable within the information that
available to me.
Dr Tolman’s view of the palliative approach is one where symptom
management is addressed aggressively with sedating medications
which are not
always the most appropriate in the circumstances. As highlighted in the cases
above on some occasions these decisions
have been contentious, and others
potentially dangerous. Dr Tolman’s approach is also to cease other
medications which are
not ‘comfort care’ medications which yet again
in some of the above cases may have caused more harm than good. Dr Tolman
not back up these positions with clear documentation of a diagnosis of dying
which leads one to feel that often these
or terminally focused
approaches are assumed to be appropriate for all palliative patients, regardless
of their needs at the time
in question.”
Dr Tolman’s Response to the Notification
242. Dr Tolman responded to allegations put to her in a letter
from AHPRA dated 21 October 2013 concerning each of the twelve patients
circumstances were subsequently considered by the Panel by letter dated 17

Tolman’s position.
It is as follows.
243. Patient 1 was an 80 year old woman from a high care
facility admitted under Dr McCallum in the early evening. He noted the patient
was a difficult historian, that the daughter was present for half of the
interview and the plan was to admit her with further family
discussions
244. Dr McCallum saw the patient later that evening and the next day
and suggested a referral to the OPU with a return to the nursing
in brief a discussion with the family and a reference to a possible malignancy
but does not provide further detail.
245. Dr Tolman has written she saw the
patient prior to admission to the OPU, that she had a detailed discussion with
the daughter
who resided some distance from the hospital. Dr Tolman obtained a
history of Patient 1 from her daughter. The poor quality of life
suffered by
Patient 1 was noted – she was immobile, doubly incontinent and dysphasic.
She says she discussed treatment options
with Patient 1’s daughter based
on Patient 1’s life since entering the nursing home in 2008. She says she
and clarified the goals of care, making certain they accorded with
Patient 1’s past wishes and the daughter’s present
wishes. She says
the daughter agreed that comfort care would be the most appropriate course of
action. She noted in the record
the patient’s GP was away.
Tolman notes that neither the OPU geriatrician Dr Skelton or Dr Chris Edwards,
the palliative care registrar, made any contact
with the family.
247. In her
response to Dr Chapman’s report, Dr Tolman says:
• every element of every discussion cannot be
documented;
• the paucity of documentation by other doctors is not
raised as an issue;
• she was the only doctor to have a detailed
discussion with the families;
• she was the only consultant to do a
goals of care document;
• whilst Dr McCallum was the admitting doctor,
the care of this patient was given to the OPU. Dr Skelton was heavily involved
in this patient’s care. While only one consultant can be listed as
“admitting doctor” she says they were very
much a team.
248. In response to Dr Chamberlen’s concerns that there
had been insufficient investigation of Patient 1’s condition,
noted the OPU team simply endorsed the goals of care plan set out by Dr
McCallum’s team and it was his team that
first thought of a possible
diagnosis of an underlying cancer and that it was his team that wrote in the
notes that further investigation
of that condition was inappropriate.
addition, Dr Tolman says in light of Patient 1’s history and the
family’s wishes the OPU team felt the management
plan was appropriate.
According to Patient 1’s daughter, her mother had poor quality of life and
the decision was made, in
conjunction with her, for Patient 1 not to have
further investigations.
250. In response to criticisms that her approach was
too “focus [sic] on what the family wanted” and that she was
to rush to judgement”, Dr Tolman said she was very focused on
the needs of acutely ill older people and that rapid, collaborative
defines best practice in the elderly.
251. Patient 2 was first seen by Dr Skelton (consultant
geriatrician) and Dr Hovinga (registrar) in November 2012. The patient’s
medical conditions were ilesostomy, worsening renal failure, oesophageal
stricture, deteriorating liver failure, declining cognition
and chronic
alcoholism. He was refusing review by a speech pathologist and his recorded
goal of care was palliative.
252. When Dr Tolman first saw this patient she
agreed with the assessment of Dr Skelton and Dr Hovinga.
253. Patient 2 was
transferred to the OPU as he was dying and everyone knew this, including the
patient and his family. With a view
to providing him the last possible chance
of survival, Dr Tolman says the OPU team agreed he would undergo oesophageal
dilation which
took place on 7 November 2012.
254. He was noted to be more
unwell the next day, his condition “fragile and precarious”. This
was not a surprise to
the treating team, however Patient 2’s wife was not
coping with his poor condition.
255. The dilations did not improve matters by
12 November 2012, parenteral fluids had been commenced on 9 November 2012.
care was begun using a syringe driver in response to his continued
decline and he died, not unexpectedly, the next day.
256. Dr Butcherine, who
disagreed with the palliative approached adopted by the team, undertook serum
electrolyte testing in the later
stages. The patient’s high sodium count
was noted on 12 November 2012 but Dr Tolman did not see this as an issue as it
often part of the dying process. She says in her letter “His high
sodium was the consequence of his dying, not the cause”.
257. Dr Tolman
disagrees with Dr Butcherine’s decision to measure the electrolytes in the
first place and also disagrees the
abnormality should have been
258. The family, the ward staff and finally even the patient
himself were all aware he was dying. Dr Tolman says “It would
crazy to interrupt good symptom control with attempted reversal of this
biochemical imbalance.”
259. Dr Tolman notes this patient “could
not eat due to oesophageal stricture which was no longer responding to
dilations, he
had a worsening liver and renal failure, he was delirious due to
his medical condition and his alcohol dependence over many years
distressed and suffering.”
260. As to the allegation of Dr Chapman of
“lack of diagnosis prior to treatment”, Dr Tolman responds by saying
diagnosis is often absent in the real worlds”.
delivery of medication by a subcutaneous route is not always terminal sedation.
This method, she says, can be carried out
for months or longer and the patient
eats as tolerated. She says terminal sedation is intense sedation which is
accompanied by the
restriction of food and fluids. She says she has never
practised terminal sedation.
262. The commencement of midazolam in a syringe
driver “is very common practice and does not put my management at odds
that of peers.”
263. This man was 84 years old and had undiagnosed dementia
prior to his admission to the OPU on 9 March 2012 with rectal bleeding.
had been no family contact, or GP contact, since 2007. His history was obtained
from his close friend.
264. He was unkempt, with offensive foul smelling
feet. He had disabling chronic back pain and was impulsive and perseverative
a Mini Mental score of 15/30. He was functionally very dependent and his
dementia would have precluded him from being able to benefit
rehabilitation. His history was obtained from his close friend who was clear
Notwithstanding that the patient died unexpectedly on the ward a few days
265. Dr Butcherine says she was surprised by his death, but Dr Tolman
says he was after all an elderly man who had not been complying
with his medical
care and presented with rectal bleeding. Natural deaths, she says, do not
warrant investigation and she later confirmed
with the Coroner’s
Associate, Mr Russell Smith, this was not a reportable death. She denies lying
to a member of the mortality
meeting about whether this matter had been referred
to the Coroner.
266. Dr Tolman says the management of older people depends on
a range of factors. As patients age, disorders of neurodegeneration
manifest. Patients are increasingly frail, cognitively impaired and often
unable to make informed decisions. She says
no neurodegenerative illness can be
cured. Diagnosis depends on a comprehensive history backed up by a thorough
examination and
targeted investigations. The goal of care may be palliation
when cure is not likely. She says best practice is to have a collaboration
the medical team obtains the information it needs to make plans and serve the
needs of the patient.

309. Writing this
very elderly woman with very significant comorbidities up for MET or
“blue” calls is not compatible
with best practice in geriatric
medicine. There are numerous academic papers that indicate this kind of
“futile” treatment
of the very elderly causes much suffering to both
the person receiving the invasive treatment and to their families.
decision to aim for comfort care was taken with all due care and after
considerable consultation with those involved in her
life. It was made in an
appropriate way by experts in the field of aged care. A MET call would have
only subjected her to more
suffering and would not have added to the length or
quality of life. MET calls, moreover, are overused in circumstances where the
treating team fails to plan effectively. Comprehensive management plans on the
OPU limited the usefulness of MET calls for any patients.

altering the aim
of care, and inflicting further suffering.

THE NEW EVIDENCE
381. The Tribunal has previously
the nature of Dr Tolman’s appeal in this Tribunal is neither an appeal in
the strict sense nor a hearing de novo. Rather it
is a hybrid whereby the
material to be considered is confined to that placed before the Panel but with
the opportunity afforded to
both parties to present additional evidence which
bears directly upon the decision of the Panel.
382. In that regard Dr
Tolman presented evidence from Clinical Associate Professor Stuart Walker, Dr
Michael Barbato and Clinical
Professor Anthony Bell. Reports of Clinical
Associate Professor Walker and Dr Barbato were tendered by consent and they were
cross-examined. Further documentation and reports were provided by Clinical
Professor Bell which were tendered and he was cross-examined.
On behalf of the
respondent an additional report was provided by Dr Michael Chapman and he was
also cross-examined.
Clinical Associate Professor Stuart Walker
383. Clinical Associate Professor Walker’s report of 18
January 2017 consists of two pages plus his 22 page curriculum vitae
consists of a cover page, a contents page and general details, his school
graduate education, his post-graduate career, publications,
career plans and
interests. He specifically comments on the records of Patients 1, 4, 8, 10 and

to take an adequate history but that

association with the patient’s highly mobile renal function and raised
urea, he believes additional consideration of these issues with determination of
an osmolality would have been useful. A particular
focus on the patient’s
volume status, and what was being done to support this, is highly relevant.
Hyponatraemia is a known
side effect of mirtazapine.
441. Dr Chapman says
Clinical Professor Bell’s comments about Dr Tolman’s approach to
Patient 2’s hypercalcaemia
have merit. The investigations performed were
appropriate. Assuming a CT CAP was performed that was an appropriate escalation
assessment given the other negative results. At less than 3mmol/L the
treatment of hypercalcaemia should have been supportive with
attention to fluid
balance, drugs that contribute to a raised calcium and mobilisation if possible.
The advent of symptomatic hypercalcaemia
may have changed the approach and a
note is made in paragraph 13.7 of the Panel’s decision of the lack of a
clear approach
to delirium. Mild hypercalcaemia can contribute to delirium and
this would have been worth considering when determining a diagnosis.
Tolman’s comments at paragraph 13.8 of the Panel’s decision
“you would have to ask the team looking after him”
Chapman’s view, difficult to interpret in this context as it seems the
hypercalcaemia remains an area of clinical
relevance during the time Dr Tolman
was this patient’s treating clinician.
442. Dr Chapman says maximising
the comfort of patients is never inappropriate and it is often that
pharmacotherapy for comfort is
provided late and when a person is considered as
actively dying to the detriment of the patient and those around him or her. Dr
Chapman agrees with Clinical Professor Bell the provision of comfort care was
appropriate for this patient. The question then focuses
more on the goals of
that therapy and how the assessments to make sure that these goals were being
met were proceeded with. He says
a more complete discussion of some of the
complexities in this case appears at pages 24 to 25 of his previous report. He
says morphine
is not the preferred first line opioid analgesic in renal failure.
This patient’s last eGFR of 45 on 12 November 2012 (grade
3 or 3a renal
impairment) would put him at risk of opioid toxicity with morphine, however many
clinicians would still be happy to
use morphine in these circumstances.
However, Dr Chapman says the use of morphine in these circumstances would
require an explicit
attention to the possible toxicities associated with this
because of ongoing and predictable renal impairment. He says it is unclear
whether this was considered in this case.

462. Dr Chapman said in his previous report at pages 34 to
36 there are a number of concerns in this case with respect to the approach
the use of analgesics. He says Clinical Professor Bell comments the use of
analgesics is appropriate in cases of this sort for
empiric management of pain,
and to reduce agitation in delirium or behavioural and psychological symptoms of
dementia. Dr Chapman
says the Panel’s comments that the escalation of
dosing was rapid without clear reference to a diagnosis of the cause of pain,
monitoring of effects of treatment with standard tools is relevant. He says
analgesic dosing depends on context, and the note
taking in this case makes it
difficult to know whether this aggressive approach was required. He also says
the usual guidelines
when opioid analgesics are required for BPSD management and
other analgesic interventions have been unsuccessful would recommend
dose of between 10-20mg of oral morphine equivalent used within a 24 hour period
(the higher limit if the patient is not
opioid naïve) which is considerably
less than the 30mg OME utilised in this case. Dr Chapman also refers to his
comments in
his previous report that the use of morphine, known to cause greater
toxicity and renal impairment, as an analgesic was not best
practice in this
case. He says it is plausible, but by no means certain, the use of morphine
(over a safer analgesic) may have added
to this patient’s agitation, and
if so it’s rapid escalation was adding further toxicity and discomfort
rather than improving
the situation. As discussed in his previous report, Dr
Chapman notes the early use of benzodiazepines, and ongoing urinary retention
may have also contributed to agitation in this case. Management of agitation in
a palliative setting for patients with dementia
is complex and whether attention
to these issues would have resulted in greater comfort or different outcomes, he
says, is speculative.
463. Dr Chapman disagrees with Clinical Professor
Bell’s position with respect to the comments made in the Panel’s
inappropriate in this case. Dr Chapman says capacity assessments
performed when there is a trigger for concern and when a decision is required.
This is due to the recognition that these
assessment imply a challenge to a
person’s status in society and therefore should be avoided when possible.
In addition, capacity
can and does change over time so assessment at the time it
is required is necessary. He says on the initial review by Dr Tolman
no clear “complex” decision that was required and still the
possibility the patient’s condition “and
capacity” may
improve, which raises questions as to why this assessment was performed so
early. He notes the Panel documented,
which was acknowledged by Clinical
Professor Bell, the documentation lacks detail as to the nature of the
contributing elements to
the assessment. Dr Chapman notes Clinical Professor
Bell’s comments about the clear evidence of this patient’s cognitive
impairment. However, cognitive impairment and decision making capacity are not
synonymous and predictable links between gross assessments
(such as the MMSE)
and capacity are highly controversial.

Dr Chapman says grieving people who are
watching their friends or family decline often need to discuss issues to do with
their loved
one’s decline many times. He agrees this does not necessarily
denote a failure of communication and often simply reflects
the distress of the
488. Clinical Professor Bell and Dr Tolman’s suggestion this
patient’s treatment by the general medical team was concerning
appropriate. Despite this Dr Chapman says there are concerns about this
patient’s care in the OPU.
489. Dr Chapman repeats his comment which is
comprehensively dealt with in his earlier report that there are concerns about
how decisions
were made and documented by Dr Tolman in this case. He notes
Clinical Professor Bell says on multiple occasions that palliative
(“terminal”) sedation was appropriate. Dr Chapman disagrees because
it is unclear what comfort was being provided through
sedation, it is unclear
that this was the clear goal that was discussed with the family and it was very
unclear this was what the
patient himself wished. If Dr Tolman believed the
patient lacked the capacity to be able to be involved in his own decision making
syringe driver some documentation of the capacity assessment
would have been
appropriate.
490. As he has previously commented in other cases, Dr Chapman
says ongoing clinical assessment, particularly of physical discomfort,
critical element of the provision of palliative care. He says care cannot be
directed towards comfort without ongoing, systematic,
documented assessment of
whether comfort is being achieved.
491. Dr Chapman says Clinical Professor Bell’s
description of the confusing discrepancy between the progress notes and the
chart is accurate however he disagrees with Clinical Professor
Bell’s interpretation of what occurred. Dr Chapman says medication
10.8 demonstrates a ceftriaxone azithromycin being given as a “stat”
order on 7 March 2012. Medication chart 10.6
demonstrates ceftriaxone
azithromycin prescribed by a clinician on 8 March 2012 and ceased the same day.
Presumably by Dr Tolman.
These antibiotics were recommenced on 9 March 2012 by
Dr MacIntyre who appears to be a member of Dr Lees’ team. This order
corresponds with Dr Lees review on 9 March 2012 when it is commented that the
family “does” want antibiotics. It seems
Clinical Professor Bell is
suggesting because doses of the (at that stage) antibiotics were not missed then
they were not “ceased”.
However, the continuity of antibiotic
provision was due to the input of Dr Lees’ team and not the intent
documented by Dr
Tolman on 8 March 2012. Dr Chapman notes he discusses this
event in his earlier report at pages 54 and 55 and other concerns relating
this decision by Dr Tolman including the extent of input of the family and the
uncertain reason for ceasing the antibiotics.
492. Dr Chapman repeats his
earlier comments, as discussed in other cases, the use of benzodiazepines and
opioids can be useful in
treating agitation in a variety of contexts for persons
with cognitive impairment. Likewise the use of opioids in particular can
useful to treat pain and dyspnoea; the latter of which this patient could have
conceivably been suffering from given the assumed
diagnosis of community
acquired pneumonia. Dr Chapman says it is uncertain from the notes from Dr
Tolman what was being treated
with these agents. As has been discussed above,
morphine is not a recommended opioid to use in patients with significant renal
impairment.
Clinical Professor Bell’s comments about the other
environmental aspects of comfort being provided for this patient are valid
Dr Chapman has assumed this would have been standard for all patients being
admitted to the OPU. He disagrees with Clinical
Professor Bell’s
assertion the antibiotics were not ceased, and, as discussed in his earlier
report at pages 54 and 55, there
is reason to believe antibiotics were an
appropriate aspect of “comfort care”. Likewise “comfort
care” needs
to be defined in references to the goals of the
patient’s network and family and it seems from the review on 9 March 2012
wanted this treatment to continue.
493. While this patient’s
ongoing decline and limited recovery from his acute condition were a potential
outcome it was too
soon on 8 March 2012 to have any confidence whether his daily
quality of life could be improved.
494. Dr Chapman notes the Panel said Dr
Tolman did not document a differential for the patient’s delirium and that
relevant. For instance, Dr Tolman’s cessation of the
patient’s antibiotics could have plausibly arose from her believing
not have clear evidence of an infection, rather than antibiotics not fitting her
belief of the goals of care. However, no
documentation which details what was
considered likely is available. Despite his lack of clarity Dr Tolman’s
comments about
the multi-factorial nature of this patient’s delirium are
well made. Delirium is often multi-factorial and a clear reversible
often not able to be found particularly within the contexts of managing people
with advanced illness.
495. Dr Chapman indicates the issues of initial
management of delirium have previously been discussed by him and are dealt with
relation to this case in particular at pages 54 to 55 of his earlier report.
He says the use of opioids and benzodiazepines can
clearly be appropriate,
however, the documentation in this case makes it difficult to determine whether
it was appropriate in this
instance. Clinical Professor Bell suggests the
patient was settled overnight due to these PRN medications though the patient
received a dose of haloperidol (1.5mg IV) at 2220 on 8 March 2012 as
charted by the ED doctor. Dr Chapman says antipsychotics are
a more assumed
first line intervention for delirium in geriatric medicine however usually at a
lower dose than was provided by the
ED doctor. This suggests the
patient’s settled night may have been slow to arrive, and may not have
been due solely to Dr
Tolman’s clinical approach. Dr Chapman repeats the
best “treatment” of delirium is to reverse the cause and for
reason it is difficult to interpret Dr Tolman’s plan for this patient to
no longer receive antibiotics given the records
which are available.
Chapman says Clinical Professor Bell documents there is clear evidence to say Dr
Tolman met with the patient and his family
and spent some period of time with
them. However, what is unclear is what Dr Tolman’s rationale for ceasing
the antibiotics
and whether this was a plan the family were aware of and
consented to. Clinical Professor Bell’s suggestion that the
recommencement
of antibiotics the following day was a rational decision by Dr
Lees and the family due to the patient’s clinical improvement
confusing. Clinical Professor Bell suggested many times that antibiotics in
this case were not ceased by Dr Tolman as there is
no record of doses being
missed. Suggesting the decision to recommence them was a rational response to
clinical change implies there
was a clear plan for their cessation.
Chapman says cessation of invasive interventions if they do not fit the goals of
a patient or family is an important aspect
of good goal focussed palliative
care. While it is plausible IV therapy could have been considered inappropriate
by the patient
and his family there is no evidence within the medical records to
suggest this is the case. As Dr Chapman discussed at page 54 of
his earlier
report Dr Lees’ response clearly implies the family did want antibiotics
to achieve their goals of care. While
the patient seemed less agitated based on
clinical descriptions the following day there is little to suggest his clinical
was otherwise remarkably different. As Dr Chapman has said previously
antibiotics were also a reasonable palliative measure for
the patient as an
infection was the most likely, easily reversible cause of his delirium.
498. With respect to this patient Clinical Professor Bell says
Dr Tolman could not have been informed about the fracture and accordingly
has been harshly judged as her clinical decisions were not “hasty”
but the result of too much trust in her junior
miscommunication.
499. In response Dr Chapman notes from the Panel’s
decision at paragraphs 18.3 to 18.7 Dr Tolman says she did not examine this
patient, assess her clinical status or comfort or read the case file prior to
discussing the course of management with her son.
Clinical Professor Bell lauds
Dr Tolman’s habit of early morning ward rounds in response to her seeing
this patient at 7:30am.
Dr Tolman’s notes suggest she was by herself when
discussing options with the patient’s son, and it may be this assessment
was done prior to her junior staff arriving. If these assumptions are correct
suggesting the issues with the patient’s care
resulted from the behaviour
of Dr Tolman’s staff, her trust in them, or their failure to inform her of
critical information
is inappropriate.
500. Dr Chapman says at page 56 of his
earlier report there are other aspects of Dr Tolman’s approach in this
case that deserve
comment. Dr Chapman challenges her assertion it was other
clinicians’ responsibility to have informed the patient’s
fracture diagnosis. His earlier report also comments on the evidence for
medical management of neck of femur fractures,
suggesting there is no clear
evidence that non-surgical management leads to better outcomes unless surgery is
clinically inappropriate.

503. Dr Chapman confirms the views set out in his

537. The Panel
found the medical notes completed by Dr Tolman were unsatisfactory in detail and
there was a lack of diagnosis prior
to the decision to treat or not to treat.
The Panel noted it recognised intervention and investigations are sometimes not
at a particular point in palliation but it never the less was
concerned about the lack of evidence, either in the medical notes or
hearing which was indicative of a thorough clinical geriatric evaluation which
included examination, assessment, possible
investigation, diagnoses, treatment
and prognoses. The Panel says a specialist geriatric physician and director of
aged care would
be expected to accept and appropriately manage complex patients
with attention to the intersection of diagnoses, their management
and quality of
life, including but not exclusively comfort, pain and dignity. A ranking of the
priorities of treatment would ensure
all possible options have been explored and
given adequate time for treatment response. It would have also ensured the
family were
making an informed decision on behalf of the patient.
538. The Panel says specialist geriatric physician skills
expected of Dr Tolman were lacking in respect of this patient. In terms
patient’s assessment the history was recorded by several members of the
team with reference to the A&E notes as
being sufficient for a geriatric
assessment. Collateral history from the patient’s friend and general
practitioner, whilst

consultation and communication with the patient and his family. A

evaluation of
this patient. The clinical examination undertaken lacked a detailed
neurological examination. An evaluation and treatment
of an ataxic gait with
falls was not addressed. Nor was there an assessment for geriatric syndromes
such as falls, malnutrition,
benzodiazepine use, social isolation and any
interventions in the patient’s care. The admission sheet states the
patient was
admitted for neurodegenerative disease but there is no evidence of
further investigations or proposed treatments.
539. Delirium and poor food
intake were documented as possible confounding issues but it appears from the

to protocols which were in place they were not found in the patient’s
medical record.

palliative care was that a patient had 6 months to live. The aim was to give

554. This patient was 89 when taken by ambulance from Compton
Downs Nursing Home to the Royal Hobart Hospital on 24 March 2012 with
confusion, drowsiness and vomiting. She was known to have Parkinson’s
disease, insulin dependent diabetes and a
history of head injury and memory loss
consistent with dementia such that family members said she was often unable to

usually alert, walked independently in a walker and was independent with
She needed help with personal care.

Hospital treatment was commenced with antibiotics, intravenous fluids and

556. On 25 March 2012
a meeting between Dr Tolman and the family led to the decision to cease all

557. The Panel found the decision by Dr Tolman to
cease all active treatment was a rapid decision made in the context of an acute
exacerbation of delirium that may have resolved with time. A urinary tract
infection was later established. There is no record
of a discussion of this
possibility with the family. Morphine was used without evidence of pain of such
severity to warrant it.
Morphine could have been expected to cause drowsiness
at the dose used, particularly as it was used with the sedative
558. While the medical record entry by Dr Tolman dated 25 March

there is no note or record to indicate any of those issues were addressed or
further evaluated for reversible interventions.

dressing and eating
which suggests a high level of functional performance in
residential care. Ceasing medications used to treat Parkinson’s disease
may have made eating and drinking difficult which may have led to her decline.
The patient was eating small quantities on 26 March
2012. There is a note by Dr
Tolman “please give plenty of break through” but it does not clarify
what symptoms were
being treated nor why “plenty” was

564. When asked
agreed it might have resolved. However
as the family indicated the patient had
done this a number of times, their view was that her quality of life did not
support the
treatment of the delirium.
565. Dr Tolman indicated when
considering comfort care over restorative care many patients did not die, but in
this particular case,

566. When asked about the starting dosages of 20mg morphine and 20mg
midazolam, Dr Tolman said the decision to begin those medications

Tolman did not believe these were high doses for subcutaneous injection. Nor
did she feel the patient was over sedated.
In her opinion the patient was

568. Dr Tolman said there were many reasons to keep
this patient comfortable and there was nothing she knew now at the hearing which
would change that view.
569. When Dr Tolman was asked for her view on whether
it was okay for the family to decide whether to treat the delirium, continue
diabetes insulin and see if the patient got back to health, when there was a
