functioning. She felt a more humanistic approach was
to treat her like a person
and not a patient.
570. The Panel found the medical notes in respect of this
patient were inadequate and unsatisfactory. They did not report relevant
details of clinical findings, information given to the patient and/or her family
and medication management.
571. Upon admission the patient was commenced on
antibiotics, with intravenous fluids and insulin for a presumptive diagnosis of
tract infection. The Goals of Care plan described the approach to her
treatment as curative and restorative with limitations. The
next day, a meeting
between Dr Tolman and the family led to a decision to cease all active
treatment, including antibiotics and fluids.
After a family meeting on the day
after that, a subcutaneous infusion of morphine and midazolam was commenced,
presumably on the
basis the family did not believe the patient would have wanted
her life to continue.
572. The Panel notes there is no record of a discussion
with the family of the test results that indicated the presence of a urinary
tract infection. There is no record suggestive of the presence of pain or
agitation warranting the use of morphine.
573. This patient presented to the Emergency Department at 0446
hours and was assessed by the emergency room doctor as having declining
mobility, falls and dementia requiring allied health input and consideration of
medication adjustment. At 1300 hours that day he
was assessed by Dr Tolman as
lacking capacity to make decisions. However exploration of reversible causes of
his decline as well
as a consideration of medication side effects or
interactions is not evident in the medical record. During the hearing Dr Tolman
discussed the anticholinergic burden of medications as being an important
consideration. However the Panel notes the medical records
do not reflect any
acknowledgement of the contribution by medications to the decline in the
patient’s condition. Cessation
of medications may have taken longer to
see the effect of any outcome.
574. Urinary retention and falls as two common
geriatric syndromes with many potential explanations and interventions were only
superficially by Dr Tolman. This was in spite of the OPU being
described by her as a comprehensive geriatric assessment ward where
multidisciplinary team members were required to perform a thorough assessment
and intervention. Troublesome delusions reported
by Dr Tolman during the
hearing of the patient being buried alive and in front of a firing squad were
not found in the medical record.
Treatment of those delusions was not clear but
the side effects of antipsychotic medications of shortening life and causing
was highlighted by Dr Tolman.
575. The daily review of every case and
review of all diagnostic tests is not evident in this patient as vitamin D
deficiency and
vitamin B12 insufficiency were not addressed, nor were the
abnormalities found on a CT brain scan. Exploration of back pain, empiric
treatment by the general practitioner with prednisolone and urinary retention
was superficial. If goals of the OPU, as described
above were for comprehensive
medical assessment with frequent reassessments for change by a senior geriatric
specialist keen to demonstrate
a high degree of intellectual rigor then these
goals were not met.
576. The death certificate indicated a lack of injury
despite the patient being found on the floor face down 6 days before death.
Guidance for junior doctors was not apparent. Geriatric pain scales, screening
for urinary retention and attention to bowel regimen
as part of a comprehensive
geriatric assessment on the OPU was neither evident in the medical records nor
did Dr Tolman confirm these
events occurred.
577. Opioid dosing commenced by
Dr Tolman on the sixth hospital day at a total of 5mg morphine which was quickly
accelerated to 25mg
in the syringe driver by the fourteenth day of the hospital
admission with 5mg as break through. The Panel found these dosages in
naïve patient were inappropriate especially given there was no assessment
of his pain nor a reference to the geriatric
pain scale which would facilitate
consistency in evaluating and treating pain. Renal function when assessed on
the third hospital
day was impaired (eGFR 32). A pharmacist’s input is
not evident.
578. The Panel also noted Dr Tolman was defensive in relation to
questions about this patient. The Panel was concerned about a lack
and reflection about these allegations on her conduct.
579. During the
hearing Dr Tolman said she could not recall the specifics of this patient but,
when given the opportunity, made no
reference either to her own notes or the
medical records which were made available to her. She mentioned the
patient’s dementia
but could not recall the other medical reasons for his
admission. She recalled the patient’s dementia was at the extreme end
the scale and his quality of life was very poor. He was experiencing delusional
pain, falling over, intermittently able to eat
and was experiencing delusions
and hallucinations. He had been an architect with a love of music and this man
had now gone.
580. When she was asked whether a delirium pathway was
undertaken, Dr Tolman said not for this patient. Generally she said old people
with dementia had a poor prognosis and their mortality was high and this was a
distressing time for the patient and their family.
Dr Tolman indicated her
approach was to determine whether it was delirium or dementia, look at the
precipitants (including the medications)
or reasons and reverse anything she
could, always in collaboration with the care team and the family. The aim was
to remove people
from their medication to try and normalise their life as much
as possible.
581. Dr Tolman said it was also important to consider the safety
of the patient and staff members. In regard to long term care plans,
explained, if it is a new thing, do what you can to get rid of the delirium.
But if it is on the back of a nasty dementia then
it is important to talk to the
family about what you are trying to achieve. The important thing was to
normalise the patient as
much as possible and treat the medical
582. If the patient is experiencing low grade delirium, Dr Tolman
would consider whether the patient had been eating. If not, she
would consider
withdrawing artificial feedings to give the patient some hope to get better and
perhaps get them out into the sunlight.
When speaking to the patients she
stressed it was important to give them hope especially when as a doctor you do
not know where
they are at.
583. Dr Tolman indicated that insofar as this

of the delirium. When asked whether a catheter might have dealt with the

it was a combination of factors which caused his
poor quality of life and that had driven his delirium and they did their best to
reverse them. She said it took time to work out what was really going

multidisciplinary team meetings. She confirmed

what they were treating.

593. The Panel found the medical notes for this patient were
“poorly lacking and unsatisfactory”. Many matters referred

602. The pathway to death by older patients is not always clear
nor progressively downward. When asked during the hearing Dr Tolman
explain the procedure for the Terminal Care Pathway other than to say that as
someone is dying you provide comfort for
the patient and family.
Tolman dismissed the use of the MET call as a second pair of eyes on the

to manage the patient’s

604. Dr Tolman
was asked why there was a discussion of transferring this patient to a nursing
sepsis and a recurrent bowel

questioned by the Panel Dr Tolman said in respect of this patient she did not
make decisions individually but that decisions
were made by the treating team.
She said Dr Butcherine had looked at the microbiology and thought the patient
was probably suffering
from sepsis. This had not been diagnosed or treated

606. Dr Tolman met the family on the Monday
following the Saturday admission to the OPU. She told the Panel during that

antibiotics were commenced by Dr Butcherine on the Saturday. They were ceased

656. The Panel found
attention to the patient’s details was lacking and should be a determinant

substantial implications of this for prognosis

Did Dr Tolman Breach the Relevant Code of Conduct
Allegation 1: Dr Tolman’s record keeping and documentation
were unsatisfactory
724. The Good Medical Practice: Code of Conduct for Doctors in
Australia provides:
8.4 Medical
Maintaining clear and accurate medical records is essential for the
continuing good care of patients. Good medical practice involves:
Keeping accurate, up-to-date and legible records that
report relevant details of clinical history, clinical findings, investigations,
information given to patients, medication and other management in a form that
can be understood by other health
practitioners.”
725. We agree with the Panel’s comment that medical
records facilitate the continuity of patient care and are to be completed
time of the events or as soon as possible thereafter.
726. The Panel noted Dr
Tolman in her written responses denied her record keeping and documentation in
relation to the twelve patients
under review was inadequate or unsatisfactory.
She made reference to generalised comments by colleagues concerning her record
many of which referred to a time prior to the establishment of the OPU.
None of the testimonials provided by Dr Tolman made reference
to the medical
notes concerning the patients under review.
727. The Panel recorded that
during the hearing Dr Tolman made reference to examinations, potential diagnoses
and prognoses and discussions
with family members and colleagues which were not,
and should have been, included in the medical notes.
728. It is noted that
the Panel members (three of whom are medical practitioners, two of which are
specialists) had the benefit of
reading the medical notes of each patient and
the opportunity to seek clarification from Dr Tolman in person. Likewise two
of the Tribunal are medical practitioners and one of whom is a
specialist. Both medical members of the Tribunal have considerable
in the care and management of geriatric patients and both have been involved in
professional conduct proceedings in Victoria
and Tasmania. All members of the
Tribunal have had the benefit of reading the medical notes, have considered Dr
Tolman’s written
responses and her responses to questions raised by the
729. It is noted that during the Panel’s hearing Dr Tolman
indicated an intern or junior doctor would accompany her on rounds
responsibility for writing medical notes. Alternatively she said she simply
forgot to write everything in the notes as
she was busy.
730. According to
both Dr Tolman and Clinical Professor Bell the culture of the hospital was one
of poor note taking but she delegated
that task to the least knowledgeable
member of the team and did not ensure the notes were checked for completeness or
731. During the hearing before the Panel Dr Tolman outlined the
daily work of the OPU was to review every patient, their medical and
conditions, all their diagnostic tests and their discharge plans as well as the
medical notes of the team. These reviews
would take place at a daily
multidisciplinary team meeting. However what took place at those meetings was
rarely reduced to writing
and reviews were not evident in the patients’
medical records.
732. The Panel comments that as a specialist medical
practitioner in charge of the OPU the public would expect her medical notes and
documentation to reflect the seniority of her position and provide an example
for other members of the team to follow.
733. The purpose of medical notes is
to enable the next treating practitioner to clearly understand what has happened
in respect of
each patient to ensure continuity of care. The Panel comments
that in the OPU environment, where there was clearly a team based
approach, this
would have been crucial due to the number of health practitioners involved in
each patient’s care.
734. The Panel found Dr Tolman did not meet this
standard. Her medical notes did not report relevant details of clinical
clinical findings, investigations, information given to patients and
their families, medication and other management of the patients.
issue was identified in relation to Patients 1, 4, 8, 10 and 11. The Panel also
identified it as an issue in relation to
Patients 2, 5 and 7.
736. In relation to Patient 1 the Panel found the medical
records were inadequate and unsatisfactory. It found they were deficient
reporting relevant details of clinical history, clinical findings,
investigations and information given to the patient and/or
the patient’s
family. There was no indication of the multidisciplinary team meetings which
reviewed the patient in her medical
737. Complex geriatric patients
may be referred to the geriatrician for comprehensive evaluation and treatment
of their past, present
and future health needs. This approach, insofar as Dr
Tolman’s record keeping was concerned, was not evident. While a history
from the patient’s daughter around the functional performance of the
patient was recorded, prior and current treatments of
medical conditions and
social determinants of health were not recorded in the assessment. Screening
for geriatric syndromes such
as falls, polypharmacy, malnutrition and vitamin
deficiencies, and delirium were not recorded. There was no record of a physical
examination of the patient by Dr Tolman.
738. During the hearing Dr Tolman
reported the patient had a malignancy that required no treatment; however the
the malignancy was not further defined nor was reference made
to it in the notes. The notes were inconsistent concerning the use
medications between the Discharge Summary and the Discharge Interim Medication
Orders for Aged Care Facilities. Dr Tolman was
not able to explain these
inconsistencies.
739. Having reviewed this matter by considering the evidence
which we have identified in this decision as relevant and of weight we
with the Panel’s findings.
740. The Panel found the medical notes completed by Dr Tolman
with respect to this patient were unsatisfactory in detail and there
of diagnosis prior to the decision to treat or not to treat. The Panel
recognises intervention and investigations are
sometimes not necessary at a
particular point in palliation but it nevertheless was concerned about the lack
of evidence, either
in the medical notes or during the hearing of a thorough
clinical geriatric evaluation, including examination, assessment, possible
investigation, diagnoses, treatment and prognoses. A ranking of the priorities
of treatment would ensure all possible options have
been explored and given
adequate time for treatment response. It would have also ensured the family
were making an informed decision
on behalf of the patient.
741. Dr Tolman
signed this patient’s death certificate and said the cause of the death
was renal failure of 3 weeks. The Panel
found a review of the medical notes
revealed that renal failure was present for at least 5 years. In addition
shortly before death
an advanced trainee postulated a number of medical
diagnoses for this patient which were not mentioned on the death
certificate.
742. The Panel found Dr Tolman breached the standard of record
keeping and documentation with respect to this patient. Having reviewed
matter by considering the evidence which we have identified in this decision as
relevant and of weight we agree.
743. The Panel found the medical notes in respect of this
patient were inadequate and unsatisfactory as they did not report relevant
details of clinical findings, information given to the patient and/or her family
and medication management. Upon admission the patient
was commenced on
antibiotics, with intravenous fluids and insulin for a presumptive diagnosis of
a urinary tract infection. The
Goals of Care plan completed describe the agreed
approach to her treatment as curative and restorative with limitations. The
day, a meeting with Dr Tolman and the family led to a decision to cease all
active treatment, including antibiotics and fluids.
After the family meeting on
the day after that, a subcutaneous infusion of morphine and midazolam was
commenced, presumably on the
basis the family did not believe the patient would
have wanted her life to continue. There is no record of a discussion with the
family of the tests results that indicated the presence of a urinary tract
infection. There is no record suggestive of the presence
of pain or agitation
warranting the use of morphine. It found the standard had been breached.
744. Having reviewed this matter by considering the evidence which we have
identified in this decision as relevant and of weight we
agree with the
Panel’s finding.
745. The Panel found the medical records were inadequate and
unsatisfactory as they were deficient in reporting relevant details of
history, clinical findings, investigations and information given to the patient
and/or the family. In addition the death
certificate completed by Dr Tolman
indicated a lack of injury despite the medical notes revealing the patient had
been found on the
floor, face down, 6 days before death. The recording of
opioid dosing commenced by Dr Tolman on day 6 of the patient’s
hospitalisation
was not clear in respect of how much medication was given,
especially via the syringe driver. In addition the patient was also taking
quetiapine and haloperidol in the syringe driver. Despite this medication there
was no pharmacy notes on the file although the medical
practitioner assured the
Panel the pharmacist was keeping an eye on medications at the MDT
746. In relation to the medical records with respect to this
patient the Panel found they were not accurate and nor did they report
details of the clinical history, findings, investigations and medications. The
Panel found Dr Tolman breached this standard.
747. Having reviewed this
matter by considering the evidence which we have identified in this decision as
relevant and of weight we
agree with the Panel’s finding.

Allegation 2: Dr Tolman’s decision making was excessively
hasty and not fully considered
761. The Panel noted that under the Code, good patient care
involves maintaining a high level of medical competence and professional
conduct. Good medical practice involves using insight and professional
judgement in a way that would meet the standards expected
by one’s peers
and by the community.
762. Under the Code care of a patient is of primary
concern. Good patient care includes:
“2.1.1 Assessing
the patient, taking into account the history, the patient’s views and an
appropriate physical examination.
The history includes relevant psychological,
social and cultural aspects.
2.1.2 Formulating and implementing a suitable management plan (including
arranging investigations and providing information, treatment
763. The Panel state good medical practice involves taking a
clinical history, making clinical findings, conducting investigations,
communicating with and providing information to patients and prescribing
medication and other care management as appropriate. It
also involves the
“2.2.4 Considering
the balance of benefit and harm in all clinical management decisions;
2.2.6 Providing treatment options based on the best available information;
2.2.7 Taking steps to alleviate patient symptoms and distress, whether or not
a cure is possible.”
764. This allegation was identified in relation to Patients 4
and 6. The Panel also identified it as an issue with respect to Patient
765. The Panel found the decision by Dr Tolman to cease all
active treatment was a rapid decision made in the context of an acute
exacerbation of delirium that may have resolved with time. The Panel found Dr
Tolman did not provide treatment options on the best
available information
(standard 2.2.6); did not consider the balance of benefit and harm in her
clinical management decisions (standard
2.2.4) and did not formulate and
implement a suitable management plan (including arranging investigations and
providing information,
treatment and advice) (standard 2.1.2). Upon the
patient’s admission treatment was commenced with antibiotics, intravenous
fluids and insulin for a presumptive diagnosis of urinary tract infection. A
Goals of Care plan completed on the day of admission
described the agreed
approach to her treatment as curative and restorative with
limitations.
766. On 25 March 2012 a meeting between Dr Tolman and the
patient’s family led to the decision to cease all active treatment,
including insulin, antibiotics and fluids. A further meeting the next day
confirmed the family did not believe the patient would
have wanted her life to
continue. A subcutaneous infusion of morphine and midazolam was commenced and
the next day the dosage increased,
although the records suggest that pain and
agitation were not present.
767. Morphine was used without evidence of pain
of such severity as to warrant its use. Morphine could have been expected to
drowsiness at the dose used, particularly as it was used with the sedative
768. A urinary tract infection was later established. There is no
record of a discussion of this possibility with the family. The
Panel found Dr
Tolman failed to inform the patient’s family of the nature of and need for
all aspects of their clinical management
(standard 3.3.3) and failed to discuss
with them the patient’s condition and the available management options,
including their
potential benefit and harm (standard 3.3.4).
769. While a
list of medical issues was noted in an entry dated 25 March 2012 none of those
issues were subsequently addressed nor
her conditions further evaluated for
reversible interventions. The Panel found Dr Tolman failed to adopt a
diagnostic approach to
identify reversible pathologies impacting on the
patient’s function (RACP) and failed to make an appropriate assessment of
the patient (standard 2.1.1).
770. Accordingly the Panel found this
allegation proved. Having reviewed this matter by considering the evidence
which we have identified
in this decision as relevant and of weight we agree
with the Panel’s finding.

783. The Royal College of Physicians, of which Dr Tolman is a

internal medicine in order to identify reversible pathologies

819. This allegation was identified in relation to Patients 3,

includes a trigger, engagement of the patient, information gathering from
collaterals, education to learn needed information, a structured
interview and a
record of the evidence. If a patient lacks capacity a valid surrogate decision
maker should be discussed.
820. The Code says good medical practice
“2.2.5 Communicating
effectively with patients.
2.1.1 Assessing the patient, taking into account the history, the
patient’s views and an appropriate physical examination. The
includes relevant psychological, social and cultural aspects.
2.1.2 Formulating and implementing a suitable management plan (including
arranging investigations and providing information, treatment
and advice).
2.2.3 Maintaining adequate records.”
821. The Panel noted that a reference to the term
“patient” in the Code also includes a substitute decision maker for
patients who do not have the capacity to make their own decisions.
822. Neither the medical records nor Dr Tolman’s evidence
at the hearing before the Panel indicated these steps were followed
of this patient. He was categorised as not having capacity on the admission
note with no further enquiry. While the
Panel found there was rapid assessment
by Dr Tolman that the patient lacked decision making capacity, which on review
be the correct decision, there were no steps taken to discuss a valid
surrogate decision maker.
823. The Tribunal has reviewed this allegation with
respect to this patient by considering the evidence which we have identified in
this decision as relevant and of weight and we agree with the Panel’s
824. The medical records in respect of this patient do not

825. The Panel found Dr Tolman failed to maintain adequate records
(standard 2.2.3).

827. The Panel noted it appears from the records the family had

Allegation 7: Whether Dr Tolman failed to make a proper
diagnosis or was inflexible in her diagnosis.
831. A specialist geriatric physician and director of aged care
is expected to accept and appropriately manage complex patients with
to the intersection of diagnoses, their management and quality of life,
including, but not exclusively, comfort, pain and
dignity. Such a physician
would also be expected to rank the priorities of treatment and ensure all
possible options have been explored
and given adequate time for a treatment
832. The Code sets out the appropriate standard of providing good
care and it is as follows:
“2.1.1 Assessing
the patient, taking into account the history, the patient’s views, and an
appropriate physical examination.
The history includes relevant psychological,
social and cultural aspects.
2.1.2 Formulating and implementing a suitable management plan (including
arranging investigations and providing information, treatment
and advice).
2.2.3 Maintaining adequate records.
2.2.4 Considering the balance of benefit and harm in all clinical management
2.2.6 Providing treatment options based on the best available information.
2.2.7 Taking steps to alleviate patient symptoms and distress, whether or not
a cure is possible.
2.4.4 Giving priority to investigating and treating patients on the basis of
clinical need and effectiveness of the proposed investigations
treatment.”
833. In caring for patients towards the end of their life, good
medical practice involves:
“3.12.1 Taking steps
to manage a patient’s symptoms and concerns in a manner consistent with
their values and wishes.”
834. The Royal College of Physicians (RACP), of which Dr Tolman
is a member, states specialists in geriatric medicine will have the
• A geriatrician has expertise in the diagnosis and

• Assess, diagnose, treat and manage acute and chronic

• Discern whether and when to order
diagnostic tests and interpret results.

was requested by the Panel to reflect on this case and she was asked whether she

differently.

860. At an initial assessment on admission on 29 July 2012
there was evidence of infection and antibiotics were commenced on the likelihood
this was respiratory. The next day blood cultures were strongly suggestive of
an intra-abdominal infection and Dr Butcherine changed
antibiotics accordingly.
Dr Tolman recorded a discussion with the family on 31 July 2012 in which she
states the problem as “probably
adhesions” and records a plan to
cease antibiotics. There is no record of the examination of the patient by Dr
reflection on the diagnostic possibilities or an acknowledgement of
the implications of the sepsis.
861. During the hearing before the Panel Dr
Tolman said she was aware of the infection and she had examined the patient.
she did not recall whether she had discussed the acute infection issue
with the family. When asked for the clinical basis for stopping
antibiotics,
whether she thought the patient was dying, the Panel records Dr Tolman was
evasive. “Is she dying right here
and now or going on to get better and
have another bout?” When asked whether she thought the patient’s
life was threatened
immediately, she responded “I can give her antibiotics
now, it might get her better. Or is it worth a potential getting better
keeping her comfortable?”
862. When asked whether she attempted to make
a diagnosis on the cause of the sepsis, Dr Tolman responded by saying she did
whether it was sepsis or not. When asked whether she thought it was
essential to know what was going on in order to know whether
to treat or not
treat, she responded the family told her the patient had been in pain for over
40 years and the family had opted
for non-invasive treatment and comfort care.
Yet when she was asked about the acceptance of end of life care, she said that a
regarding transfer to a nursing home was had as the patient might
have gotten better unexpectedly.
863. The Panel found no diagnosis of the
sepsis was made. There is no evidence of a terminal care pathway which Dr
Tolman had said
would be instituted within a few days of somebody dying. The
cessation of antibiotics did not appear to be based on a formal end
of life care
plan but on a decision made by the family the patient was suffering from long
term pain and the prospects of curing
864. The Panel found Dr
Tolman failed to provide treatment options based on the best available
information (standard 2.2.6), failed
to take steps to alleviate patient symptoms
and stress, whether or not a cure is possible (standard 2.2.7) and failed to
adequate records (standard 2.2.3). The Panel said a geriatrician is
expected to adopt a diagnostic approach to the practice of acute
medicine in order to identify reversible pathologies impacting on a
patient’s functioning (RACP). In order for the
family to make decisions
on behalf of the patient they must be informed of the known facts. Dr Tolman
must therefore make a real
attempt to obtain all the facts, communicate them
effectively, acknowledge limitations and use them in conversation with the
to achieve an appropriate treatment decision.
865. The Tribunal has
reviewed this allegation with respect to this patient by considering the
evidence which we have identified in
this decision as relevant and of weight and
we agree with the Panel’s finding.

4.4.3 Communicating effectively with other team
875. The Royal College of Physicians (RACP), of which Dr Tolman
is a member, states that specialists in Geriatric Medicine will have
following expertise:
• A geriatrician has expertise in the diagnosis and
management of complex and/or multi factorial internal medicine disorders
impacting on the cognition and functional status of the older person.
• A geriatrician adopts a diagnostic approach to the practice of acute
internal medicine in order to identify reversible pathologies
impacting on a
patient’s function, psychological and social wellbeing.
876. In addition, a geriatrician is expected to:
• Assess, diagnose, treat and manage acute and chronic
illnesses in older people.
• Discern whether and when to order
diagnostic tests and interpret results.
877. Complex geriatric patients such as those that make their
way to this acute aged care ward are referred to a geriatrician for
comprehensive evaluation and treatment of their past, present and future health
needs. This specialist approach, the Panel found,
was lacking in respect of
this patient. While abnormalities in her condition were noted in the medical
records and investigations,
there was no action by Dr Tolman to facilitate a
diagnosis, a prognosis or a treatment plan prior to treatment. A diagnostic
making process is necessary, whether or not the interventions are
appropriate, and needs to be recorded.
878. The Panel found Dr Tolman failed
to assess, diagnose, treat and manage this patient or to discern whether and
when to organise
diagnostic tests (standards 2.2.1 and 2.2.7).
Tribunal has reviewed this allegation with respect to this patient by
considering the evidence which we have identified in
this decision as relevant
and of weight and we agree with the Panel’s finding.
880. Dr Tolman noted this patient had “little prospects
for recovery relate to time rather than active process”. She
explained in
the hearing before the Panel the patient’s deterioration was not going to
happen acutely but over time, and his
prospects of recovery were more likely if
allowed time rather than doing a number of tests and investigations. When asked
high calcium levels and what was being done to address this during the 11
or so days of care Dr Tolman deferred to the treating team
“You’d have to ask the team looking after him”.
Panel was not satisfied that the treatment decision which was effectively
“wait and see” was well founded on
medical grounds. The Panel found
Dr Tolman failed to assess, diagnose, treat and manage this patient or to
discern whether and when
to order diagnostic tests (standards 2.2.1 and
882. The Panel also found Dr Tolman failed to communicate effectively
with other team members. Working in a team does not alter a
personal accountability for professional conduct and the care provided
(standards 4.4 and 4.4.3).
883. The Tribunal has reviewed this allegation
with respect to this patient by considering the evidence which we have
identified in
this decision as relevant and of weight and we agree with the
Panel’s finding.
884. The Panel found the attention to the patient’s
details was lacking and should have been a determinant of a comprehensive
geriatric assessment. There was no evidence Dr Tolman undertook appropriate
assessments, diagnoses, prognoses or treatment options.
There was no evidence
as a senior geriatric consultant she reviewed the treating teams notes to
formulate the best management plan
for the patient. The Panel found as a result
of the failure to make an appropriate assessment, Dr Tolman failed to formulate
implement a suitable management plan (standards 2.1.1 and
885. The Panel also found Dr Tolman failed to adopt a diagnostic
approach to the practice of acute internal medicine in order to identify
reversible pathologies impacting on the patient’s functioning, contrary to
the specialist standard expected of a geriatrician
by the RACP. The Panel found
Dr Tolman failed to comply with the standards 2.2.4 and 2.2.6.
Tribunal has reviewed this allegation with respect to this patient by
considering the evidence which we have identified in
this decision as relevant
and of weight and we agree with the Panel’s finding.
DISPOSITION
887. At paragraph 37 of its decision, the Panel found, given
its findings, Dr Tolman had behaved in a way that constitutes unsatisfactory
professional performance. Section 191(1)(b)(i) of the Schedule to the National
Law permits a panel, after hearing a matter about
a registered health
practitioner, to make that decision.
888. Unsatisfactory professional
performance of a registered health practitioner is defined in s5 of the Schedule
to the National
Law as meaning:
knowledge, skill or judgment possessed, or care exercised by, the practitioner
in the practice of the health profession
in which the practitioner is registered
is below the standard reasonably expected of a health practitioner of an
equivalent level
of training or experience.”
889. Clearly that determination was open to the Panel on the
evidence it had before it, and which it took into account, and the findings
made. The Panel’s determination in that regard was not, in our view,
impugned by any of the additional evidence placed
before the Tribunal from
Clinical Professor Bell, Dr Barbato and Dr Walker. In our view the finding is
supported by the careful,
detailed and objective analysis of Dr Chapman in his
further analysis of the patients’ records and in his very careful analysis
of Clinical Professor Bell’s evidence.
890. Accordingly the Tribunal
finds the Panel’s finding in this regard was the correct and preferable
result in respect of the
matters to which the Panel’s decision
891. Where a panel decides a registered health practitioner has
behaved in a way that constitutes unsatisfactory professional performance,
s191(3) of the Schedule to the National Law permits the panel to do one or more
of the following:
“(a) impose
conditions on the practitioner’s...registration, including, for example,
in relation to a practitioner –
(i) a condition requiring the practitioner to complete specified further
education or training within a specified period; or
(ii) a condition requiring the practitioner to undertake a specified period
of supervised practice; or
(iii) a condition requiring the practitioner to do, or refrain from doing,
something in connection with the practitioner’s practice;
(iv) a condition requiring the practitioner to manage the
practitioner’s practice in a specified way; or
(v) a condition requiring the practitioner to report to a specified person at
specified times about the practitioner’s practice;
(vi) a condition requiring the practitioner not to employ, engage or
recommend a specified person, or class of persons;
(b) for a health panel, suspend the practitioner’s or student’s
registration;
(c) for a performance and professional standards panel, caution or reprimand
the practitioner.”
892. Section 191(4) of the Schedule to the National Law
“If a panel decides
to impose a condition on a registered health
practitioner’s...registration, the panel must also decide
a review period
for the condition.”
893. In this case the Panel determined it would reprimand Dr
Tolman under s191(3)(c) and impose a number of conditions on her registration
under s191(3)(a) which were as follows:
“1. The medical
practitioner must provide to the Medical Board of Australia (the Board) in
writing within seven (7) days of
these conditions being
name and address of each and every place(s) of employment as a medical
practitioner; and
each employer is aware of the conditions imposed on the practitioner’s
registration.
medical practitioner must notify the Board within seven (7) business days of any
change in employer in accordance with condition

Komesaroff (Vic) within two (2) months from the date
the conditions are imposed

any other professional
colleagues he nominates) that will include, but not be

involved in the

practitioner must submit the educational session plan as proposed by Professor

897. Dr Tolman wishes to be
heard with respect to penalty before that is determined. This was so because it
was submitted the terms
of the findings are relevant to penalty. The
Tribunal’s findings are in very similar terms to that of the Panel. The
according to her counsel, for hearing the practitioner as to penalty
therefore does not exist. In addition, Dr Tolman has previously
been given the
opportunity to fully put her case to the Panel. The Tribunal’s role is to
determine whether the Panel’s
decision was, in regard to all aspects of
it, including penalty, the correct and preferable result; it is not to embark on
898. The Panel, on finding Dr Tolman had behaved in a way
that constitutes unsatisfactory professional performance, had two options
s191(3)(c) which was either to caution or reprimand Dr Tolman. In making orders
under the National Law it is important to
understand the Tribunal’s
primary purpose is protection of the public and the Tribunal makes orders with
the purpose of maintaining
standards of and public confidence in the medical
profession. As the Court said in Craig v The Medical Board of South Australia
[2001] SASC 169
purpose of disciplinary proceedings is to protect the public, not to punish a
practitioner in the sense in which punishment
is administered pursuant to the
criminal law. A disciplinary tribunal protects the public by making orders
which will prevent persons
who are unfit to practise from practising, or by
making orders which will secure the maintenance of proper professional
A disciplinary tribunal will also consider the protection of the
public, and of the relevant profession, by making orders which
will assure the
public that appropriate standards are being maintained within the relevant
profession.”
899. The ordinary meaning of the words “caution”
on the one hand and “reprimand” on the other leads the Tribunal
the conclusion that a reprimand is an official rebuke for past conduct whereas a
caution is a reminder to take care in the future
and not to repeat the conduct.
In our view a reprimand can cause more serious consequences. In Peeke v Medical
commented on a submission to the effect that to impose a reprimand was to
trivialise a serious lapse in professional standards.
In reply his Honour said
he did not agree a reprimand is trivial. It may be inappropriate or inadequate
in many circumstances,
but a reprimand to a professional person has the
potential to result in serious adverse implications. In Honey v Medical
Practitioners
Board of Victoria
[2007] VCAT 526
the Tribunal said the following
“In our view, the order to reprimand will have
the salutary effect of underscoring the seriousness of the doctor’s
It will remind him lest he is tempted again. It will flag to others
practising the profession of psychiatry that the behaviour he
has engaged in is
to be condemned. And it will give some comfort to members of the public that
behaviour of this sort, when uncovered,
will be outed for what it is-a flagrant
violation of the fundamental principles of the doctor patient
relationship.”
900. We agree with the comments in the cases set out in
paragraph 899. In our view given the nature of the allegations and the findings
made a caution would trivialize Dr Tolman’s conduct whereas a reprimand
would not. A reprimand is therefore appropriate.
901. In relation to the
conditions the Panel directed they be reviewed by the Board at the expiration of
12 months from the date of
their imposition. They required the development of a
supervised educational plan and a program with a mentor. The educational plan
was to cover the areas in condition 3 whereas the mentoring program consisted of
the matters set out in conditions 17-20. Given
the findings of the Panel which
this Tribunal has endorsed we do not believe the conditions are unwarranted.
They might be onerous
but their purpose is to secure the maintenance of proper
professional standards. We believe their imposition will achieve that
902. The Tribunal therefore confirms the Panel’s decision
pursuant to s202(1)(a) of the Schedule to the National Law. The stay
the Tribunal on 2 May 2016 with respect to the operation of the Panel’s
conditions is lifted. That stay was granted
on the conditions set out in
paragraph 895. The removal of the stay also removes those conditions. It is
not appropriate those
conditions remain given the reference therein to the stay
which has been lifted. In addition those conditions operate in a similar
to the first two conditions imposed by the Panel. Finally, we note the
conditions imposed by the Tribunal when the stay was
granted were not part of
the Panel’s decision.
